The net proceeds from the sale of the Bonds will be used to finance: (i) the construction of the City’s New Drinking Water Facility 
Project; (ii) the payment of the issuance costs of the Bonds; and, to the extent additional proceeds are available, (iii) the payment of capital 
costs related to land, facilities, and rights constructed, installed, purchased, and otherwise acquired for the System.
The Bonds are special and limited revenue obligations of the Enterprise payable solely out of and secured by an irrevocable pledge 
of and lien (but not necessarily an exclusive lien) upon the Pledged Revenues.  The Bonds do not constitute a debt or indebtedness of the 
Enterprise or the City within the meaning of any constitutional, Charter or statutory provision or limitation.  The Bonds are not payable in 
whole or in part from the proceeds of general property taxes or any other funds of the City or the Enterprise, except the Pledged Revenues, 
and the full faith and credit of the City is not pledged for payment of the principal of or interest on the Bonds. 
The Bonds are subject to redemption prior to maturity as described herein.

INTRODUCTION 
This Official Statement is furnished to prospective purchasers of $186,900,000 Water and 
Wastewater Revenue Bonds, Series 2024 (the “Bonds”), issued by the City of Westminster, Colorado (the 
“City” or “Westminster”) Water and Wastewater Utility Enterprise (the “Enterprise”).  The offering of the 
Bonds is made only by way of this Official Statement, which supersedes any other information or materials 
used in connection with the offer or sale of the Bonds.  This Official Statement speaks only as of its date, 
and the information contained herein is subject to change. 
The following introductory material is only a brief description of, and is qualified by, the more 
complete information contained throughout this Official Statement.  A full review should be made of the 
entire Official Statement and the documents summarized or described herein.  For definitions of capitalized 
terms used and not otherwise defined in this section and elsewhere in this Official Statement see 
“APPENDIX A.” 
Issuer ......................................  Westminster is a home rule municipality that encompasses approximately 
34 square miles and has an estimated population of 114,875.  The City is 
located approximately two miles from the northwestern boundary of the 
City and County of Denver, Colorado.  See “APPENDIX D—The City” 
and the preceding “CITY MAP.” 
The Enterprise .......................  The City’s Enterprise, created by Ordinance No. 2264, Series of 1994, as 
amended by Ordinance No. 3758, Series of 2014 (the “Enterprise 
Ordinance”), consists of the City’s Water Facilities (as defined in 
Appendix A hereto), Wastewater Facilities (as defined in Appendix A 
hereto), and Stormwater Facilities.  The Wastewater Facilities are also 
referred to in City documentation as “sewer” facilities and the terms 
“wastewater” and “sewer”, in connection with system descriptions, are 
used interchangeably herein.  The Enterprise is authorized to issue its own 
revenue bonds and to enter into contracts relating to the Water Facilities 
and Wastewater Facilities of the City owned and operated by the City as a 
single utility system (the “System”).  The Enterprise, which also includes 
the City’s stormwater utility, was established to constitute an “enterprise” 
within the meaning of Article X, Section 20 of the Colorado Constitution 
(“TABOR”).  The financial activity of the Enterprise and net income 
pledged to the payment of the Bonds (as described herein) are accounted 
for as a proprietary fund of the City titled “Water and Wastewater Utility 
Fund.”  However, net income pledged to the payment of the Bonds does 
not include the financial activity of the City’s stormwater revenue utility.  
See “THE SYSTEM—The Enterprise.” 
Security ..................................  The Bonds are special and limited revenue obligations of the Enterprise 
payable solely out of and secured by an irrevocable pledge of and lien (but 
not necessarily an exclusive lien) on certain net revenues (the “Pledged 
Revenues”), which consist of all Income remaining after the deduction of 
Operation and Maintenance Expenses.    
Income generally includes (i) all income from rates, fees, tolls, and 
charges and tap fees, or any combination thereof, but not special 
assessments, for the services furnished by or the use of the System; and 2 
(ii) income or other gain from the investment of Pledged Revenues and of 
the proceeds of any security secured by a lien on the Pledged Revenues.   
Operation and Maintenance Expenses generally include reasonable and 
necessary current expenses of the City, paid or accrued, of operating, 
maintaining and repairing the System as may be determined by the City.  
See “THE BONDS—Security for the Bonds” and “THE SYSTEM.”  
Income and Operation and Maintenance Expenses are more specifically 
defined in APPENDIX A hereto.   
The Bonds are secured by an irrevocable pledge of and lien (but not 
necessarily an exclusive lien) upon the Pledged Revenues derived from 
the System, all as more particularly set forth herein and in the ordinance 
of the Enterprise providing for the issuance of the Bonds (the “Bond 
Ordinance”).  The Bonds do not constitute a debt or indebtedness of the 
Enterprise or the City within the meaning of any applicable constitutional, 
Charter or statutory provision or limitation.  The Bonds are not payable in 
whole or in part from the proceeds of general property taxes or any other 
funds of the City or the Enterprise except the Pledged Revenues, and the 
full faith and credit of the City is not pledged for payment of the principal 
of or interest on the Bonds.  See “THE BONDS—Security for the Bonds.” 
The Bonds are being issued on a parity with the City’s (i) Water and 
Wastewater Utility Enterprise, Water and Wastewater Revenue Bonds, 
Series 2016 (the “2016 Bonds”), (ii) Water and Wastewater Utility 
Enterprise, Water and Wastewater Revenue Bonds, Series 2019 (the “2019 
Bonds”), (iii) Water And Wastewater Utility Enterprise, Water and 
Wastewater Revenue Refunding Bonds, Series 2020 (the “2020 Bonds”), 
and (iv) a 2020 loan made to the City by the Colorado Water Resources 
and Power Development Authority (the “CWRPDA”) and evidenced by a 
governmental agency bond (the “2020 CWRPDA Loan” and, together 
with the 2016 Bonds, the 2019 Bonds, and the 2020 Bonds, the 
“Outstanding Parity Obligations”).   
Additionally, the CWRPDA has made a loan to the City, evidenced by the 
City’s Subordinate Water and Wastewater Revenue Bond, Series 2005 
(the “2005 Bond”), that is secured by a pledge and/or lien on the Pledged 
Revenues which is subordinate to the lien thereof of the Bonds (the 
“Subordinate Securities”).  See “THE SYSTEM—Outstanding Financial 
Obligations” and “THE BONDS—Debt Service Coverage.” 
The City and the Enterprise are prohibited from issuing securities in the 
future with a lien on the Pledged Revenues that is senior or superior to that 
of the Bonds and the Outstanding Parity Obligations.  The Bond Ordinance 
provides conditions for the issuance of one or more series of additional 
bonds, notes, interim securities or other obligations (a) having a lien on 
the Pledged Revenues which is on a parity with, but not senior or superior 
to, the lien of the Bonds and the Outstanding Parity Obligations (“Parity 
Bonds”); and (b) having a lien on the Pledged Revenues which is 
subordinate or junior to the lien of the Bonds (“Subordinate Securities”).  
See APPENDIX A under the caption “SUMMARY ORDINANCE 3 
PROVISIONS—Priorities; Liens; Issuance of Parity Bonds—Issuance of 
Parity Bonds” for a description of the conditions for the issuance of Parity 
Bonds and Subordinate Securities.   
Purpose ..................................  The net proceeds from the sale of the Bonds will be used to finance: (i) the 
construction of the City’s New Drinking Water Facility Project; (ii) the 
payment of the issuance costs of the Bonds; and, to the extent additional 
proceeds are available, (iii) the payment of capital costs related to land, 
facilities, and rights constructed, installed, purchased, and otherwise 
acquired for the System.  See “THE BONDS—Application of Bond 
Proceeds.”

hereof.  Interest on the Bonds is payable semiannually on June 1 and 
December 1 each year, commencing on December 1, 2024.  Payments to 
Beneficial Owners will be made as described in “APPENDIX G—
Book-Entry-Only System.”

date for the Bonds.  See “APPENDIX A.”
Book-Entry- 
Only Registration ..................  The Bonds will be issued in fully registered form and will be registered 
initially in the name of “Cede & Co.” as nominee for The Depository Trust 
Company, New York, New York (“DTC”), a securities depository.  
Beneficial ownership interests in the Bonds may initially be acquired in 
denominations of $5,000 in principal amount or integral multiples of 
$5,000 in excess thereof through brokers and dealers who are, or who act 
through, participants in the DTC system (the “Participants”).  Such 
beneficial ownership interests will be recorded on the records of the 
Participants.  Persons for whom Participants acquire interests in the Bonds 
(the “Beneficial Owners”) will not receive certificates evidencing their 
interests in the Bonds so long as DTC or a successor securities depository 
acts as the securities depository with respect to the Bonds.  So long as DTC 
or its nominee is the registered owner of the Bonds, payments of principal 
and interest on the Bonds, as well as notices and other communications 
made by or on behalf of the City pursuant to the Bond Ordinance will be 
made to DTC or its nominee only.  Disbursement of such payments, 
notices, and other communications by DTC to Participants, and by 
Participants to the Beneficial Owners, is the responsibility of DTC and the 
Participants pursuant to rules and procedures established by such entities.  
See “APPENDIX G—Book-Entry-Only System” for a discussion of the 
operating procedures of the DTC system with respect to payments, 
registration, transfers, notices, and other matters.  Except as otherwise 
provided herein, the term “Owner” shall refer to the registered owner of 
any Bond, as shown by the registration books maintained by U.S. Bank 
Trust Company, National Association, as Bond Registrar (the 
“Registrar”). 4

redemption prior to their respective maturity dates.  The Bonds maturing on and after December 1, 2035 
are subject to redemption prior to maturity at the option of the City, in whole or in part from any maturity 
and interest rate, in any order of maturity, by lot within a maturity and interest rate, in such manner as the 
Enterprise may determine (giving proportionate weight to Bonds in denominations larger than $5,000) on 
December 1, 2034, or any date thereafter, at a price equal to the principal amount so redeemed plus accrued 
interest to the redemption date without a redemption premium.

Redemption Procedures for the Bonds .  Notice of any redemption of Bonds, which may be a 
conditional notice, will be given by the Paying Agent in the name of the City by sending a copy of such 
notice by electronic means to DTC or by first class, postage prepaid mail, not less than 30 days prior to the 
redemption date, to the Owner of each Bond being redeemed.  Such notice should specify the number or 
numbers of the Bonds so to be redeemed (if redemption shall be in part) and the redemption date.  If any 
Bond has been duly called for redemption and if, on or before the redemption date, there will have been 
deposited with the Paying Agent in accordance with the Bond Ordinance funds sufficient to pay the 
redemption price of such Bond on the redemption date, then such Bond will become due and payable at 
such redemption date, and from and after such date interest will cease to accrue thereon.  Failure to deliver 
any redemption notice or any defect in any redemption notice will not affect the validity of the proceeding 
for the redemption of Bonds with respect to which such failure or defect did not occur.  Any Bond redeemed 
prior to its maturity by prior redemption or otherwise shall not be reissued and will be cancelled. 
Notice of Redemption .  In the event any of the of the Bonds or portions thereof are called for 
redemption as described above, notice thereof identifying the Bonds or portions thereof to be redeemed is 
to be given by the Registrar by mailing a copy of the redemption notice by first class mail (postage prepaid) 
or by electronic means, not less than 30 days prior to the date fixed for redemption, to the Owner of each 
Bond to be redeemed in whole or in part at the address shown on the registration books maintained by or 
on behalf of the City by the Registrar.  The redemption of the Bonds may be contingent or subject to such 
conditions as may be specified in the notice.  Failure to give such notice by mailing to any Owner, or any 
defect therein, will not affect the validity of any proceeding for the redemption of other Bonds as to which 
no such failure or defect exists.  All Bonds so called for redemption will cease to bear interest after the 
specified redemption date, provided funds for their redemption are on deposit at the place of payment at 
that time. 
Application of Bond Proceeds 
The Project. The City is committed to providing affordable, high-quality water services.  The 
City’s Semper and Northwest water treatment facilities currently supply more than nine billion gallons of 
clean and safe drinking water annually through over 500 miles of pipes to the City’s residents and 
businesses.  In 2015, the City began planning the construction of a new drinking water treatment facility, 
and associated raw and finished water pipelines, referred to as the New Drinking Water Facility Project 
(the “Project”).  The City Council approved the construction of the Project after evaluating community 
concerns, reviewing many significant cost savings measures and discussing various options to provide 
clean, safe, and affordable water.  The Project is a cost-effective, affordable solution with the flexibility to:  
replace and expand, if needed, aging infrastructure; respond to future State and Federal clean water 
regulations; and build additional treatment processes to respond to potential future wildfire contamination, 
significantly decrease concentrations of certain harmful chemicals, and possibly address future emerging 
contaminants.  Initial planning efforts for the Project focused on rightsizing an initial conceptual 30 Million 
Gallon per Day (“MGD”) treatment facility and associated pipelines, which had an inflation-adjusted cost 
of over $300 million.  Once the facility was rightsized to 14.7 MGD, City Staff was able to reduce the 
Project’s costs by over $100 million.  The 14.7 MGD facility will work in concert with the existing 
Northwest Water Treatment Facility and Semper Water Treatment Facility to meet the City’s water 
demands.  Additional expansions of the 14.7 MGD facility are currently planned to occur in one or more 
phases over the next 20 years.  
Preliminary cost estimates for constructing the 14.7 MGD facility are approximately $210 million, 
which includes approximately $20 million for ozonation treatment.  In addition to the 14.7 MGD facility, 
raw and finished water pipelines are estimated to cost another $50 million.  The total estimated cost of the 
Project is $260 million, of which approximately $50 million is currently expected to be paid with legally 
available City funds.  The final cost for the Project may range from up to 10% less to 20% more (which is 7 
currently anticipated to be covered with additional cash reserves, if needed) than this preliminary estimate, 
based on the 60% level of design plans under review.  As design progresses, the estimate will become more 
certain and will be adjusted to market conditions and prevailing construction valuation.  The 60% level 
design plans are currently under review and the City will receive the final updated cost estimate in the 
fourth quarter of 2024.  The final design and Guaranteed Maximum Price contract are scheduled for the 
second quarter of 2025, with construction to begin shortly thereafter.  
A portion of the Bond proceeds will be used to pay the costs of issuing the Bonds.  In the event of 
savings related to currently anticipated Project costs or material delays associated with the Project, Bond 
proceeds may be applied to capital costs related to land, facilities, and rights constructed, installed, 
purchased, and otherwise acquired for the System.

Security for the Bonds 
Special Revenue Obligations.  All of the Debt Service Requirements of the Bonds are payable 
solely out of the Pledged Revenues.  The Owners of the Bonds may not look to the General Fund or any 
other revenue or fund of the City for the payment of the Debt Service Requirements thereof, except the 
special funds pledged therefor.  The Bonds do not constitute a debt or indebtedness of the Enterprise or the 
City within the meaning of any constitutional, Charter or statutory provision or limitation, and the Bonds 
are not considered or held to be general obligations of the City but constitute special and limited obligations 
of the Enterprise.  The Bonds are not payable in whole or in part from the proceeds of general property 
taxes or any other funds of the City or the Enterprise except the Pledged Revenues, and the full faith and 
credit of the City is not pledged for payment of the Bonds.  
Definition of Pledged Revenues.  The Bond Ordinance defines the Pledged Revenues as all Income 
remaining after the deduction of Operation and Maintenance Expenses of the System.  Income generally 
includes (i) income from rates, fees, tolls, and charges and tap fees, or any combination thereof, but not 
special assessments, for the services furnished by, or the direct or indirect connection with, or the use of 
the System; and (ii) all income or other gain from the investment of Pledged Revenues and of the proceeds 
of other securities payable from and secured by a lien on the Pledged Revenues (“Securities”) (except 
income or other gain from any investment of moneys held in an escrow or similar fund for the defeasance 
of Securities) to the extent not required to be rebated to the federal government.  Operation and Maintenance 
Expenses generally include reasonable and necessary current expenses of the City, paid or accrued, of 
operating, maintaining and repairing the System as may be determined by the City.  See “THE BONDS—
Security for the Bonds” and “THE SYSTEM.”  Income and Operation and Maintenance Expenses are more 
specifically defined in APPENDIX A hereto.  8 
Additional Bonds.  The Bond Ordinance provides conditions for the issuance of one or more series 
of additional bonds, warrants, notes, leases or any other evidence of the advancement of money to the 
Enterprise or the City (a) having a lien on the Pledged Revenues which is on a parity with, but not senior 
or superior to, the lien of the Bonds and the Outstanding Parity Obligations (“Parity Bonds”) or (b) having 
a lien on the Pledged Revenues which is subordinate or junior to the lien of the Bonds (“Subordinate 
Securities”).  Superior securities may not be issued pursuant to the terms of the Bond Ordinance.  Such 
historic coverage test generally requires Pledged Revenues for the last complete Fiscal Year prior to the 
issuance of the proposed Parity Bonds, as certified by the Treasurer, to be in an amount equal to at least 1.2 
times the Combined Maximum Annual Debt Service Requirements of the Bonds then Outstanding, any 
Parity Bonds then Outstanding and the Parity Bonds proposed to be issued.  At such time as the 2016 Bonds, 
the 2019 Bonds, and the 2020 Bonds are no longer Outstanding, additional Parity Obligations may be issued 
so long as the Pledged Revenues derived in any consecutive 12 month period within the 18 months 
immediately preceding the date of issuance of the additional Parity Obligations is not less than 1.2 times 
the Combined Maximum Annual Debt Service Requirements of any Outstanding Parity Bonds and the 
Parity Bonds proposed to be issued, and 1.0 times the debt service requirements of any Subordinate 
Securities then due and owing, and the preceding test will no longer apply.  The test does permit, among 
other things, the calculation of the Pledged Revenues to be adjusted to reflect recent rate increases which 
have not been in effect throughout the calculation period.  The test for Parity Bonds under the Bond 
Ordinance is set forth in APPENDIX A under the caption “SUMMARY OF ORDINANCE 
PROVISIONS—Priorities; Liens; Issuance of Parity Bonds—Issuance of Parity Bonds.”   
For so long as the 2020 CWRPDA Loan remains outstanding, the City is also required to meet the 
coverage test provided by the loan agreement for that obligation (the “2020 Loan Agreement”) prior to 
issuing additional Parity Bonds.  The 2020 Loan Agreement requires the City to certify to CWRPDA that 
Pledged Revenues (identified in the 2020 Loan Agreement as “Net Revenues”) for any 12 consecutive 
months out of the 18 months preceding the month in which the proposed additional obligations are to be 
issued is at least equal to the sum of (a) 1.1 times the maximum annual debt service of (i) the 2020 
CWRPDA Loan and all Parity Bonds then outstanding, and (ii) such proposed obligations to be issued, and 
(b) 1.0 times the maximum annual debt service of all outstanding Subordinate Securities.   
Rate Maintenance Covenant.  The City covenants in the Bond Ordinance to prescribe, revise, and 
collect water and sewer rates, fees, tolls, and charges and tap fees or any combination thereof, to produce 
Income sufficient, together with any other moneys legally available therefor and deposited in the Water and 
Wastewater Utility Fund, to make the payments and accumulations required by the Bond Ordinance and to 
produce Pledged Revenues each Fiscal Year sufficient to, among other things, repay the Bonds.  The Bond 
Ordinance rate covenant requires coverage of 1.1 times for the Bonds, the Outstanding Parity Obligations, 
and any additional Outstanding Parity Bonds and coverage of 1.0 times for the Subordinate Securities; 
however, the documents authorizing substantially all of the Subordinate Securities contain a rate covenant 
requirement of 1.1 times coverage for said bonds.  Additionally, the Bond Ordinance rate covenant requires 
coverage of any amounts required to meet then existing deficiencies pertaining to any fund relating to the 
Pledged Revenues or any Securities payable therefrom.  See APPENDIX A under the caption “SUMMARY 
OF ORDINANCE PROVISIONS—Covenants” for a complete restatement of the rate maintenance 
covenant.    
Water and Wastewater Utility Fund and Establishment of Accounts .  The financial activities of 
the Enterprise, and the reporting and recording of the Pledged Revenues, are accounted for in a proprietary 
fund of the City titled the “Water and Wastewater Utility Fund.”  The Bond Ordinance establishes and 
identifies various accounts and subaccounts within the Water and Wastewater Utility Fund through the 
following defined terms:  the Operation and Maintenance Fund, the Principal and Interest Fund, the Reserve 
Fund, and the Rebate Fund.  Deposits or credits to the various accounts and subaccounts are made on a 
priority basis set forth below under the caption “—Flow of Funds.” 9 
Operation and Maintenance Fund.  The accounts and subaccounts defined in the Bond Ordinance 
as the Operation and Maintenance Fund are used for the recording and reporting of Operation and 
Maintenance Expenses of the System. 
Principal and Interest Fund.  The accounts and subaccounts defined in the Bond Ordinance as the 
Principal and Interest Fund are used for the recording and reporting of the Debt Service Requirements on 
the Bonds, the Outstanding Parity Obligations and any future Parity Bonds.  Pursuant to the Bond 
Ordinance, monthly deposits of Pledged Revenues are to be made to the Principal and Interest Fund, after 
making any deposits required for the Refunded Bonds, the Outstanding Parity Obligations pursuant to the 
ordinances authorizing the issuance of the Bonds and the Outstanding Parity Obligations, and concurrently 
with any amounts required by an ordinance authorizing the issuance of Parity Bonds, (i) in equal monthly 
installments the amounts necessary, together with any moneys from time to time available therefor from 
whatever source, to pay the next maturing installment of interest on the Bonds then Outstanding; and (ii) in 
equal monthly installments the amounts necessary, together with any other moneys from time to time 
available therefor from whatever source, to pay the next installment of principal of the Bonds coming due 
at maturity.   
Reserve Fund.  The City does not anticipate the creation of a reserve fund with respect to the 
Bonds.   
Rebate Fund.  The Rebate Fund is established for the purpose of accounting for required arbitrage 
rebate moneys, if any, due to the federal government under sections 103 and 148(f)(2) of the Tax Code.  
All amounts in the Rebate Fund are held free and clear of any lien created by the Bond Ordinance and, to 
the extent such amounts are required to be paid over to the federal governments, are no longer Pledged 
Revenues. 
Flow of Funds .  The City is to deposit to the Water and Wastewater Utility Fund all Income 
immediately upon receipt.  As a first charge on the Water and Wastewater Utility Fund, the City is to pay 
the Operation and Maintenance Expenses of the System as they become due and payable.  Thereafter City 
is to apply the Pledged Revenues in the following order of priority: 
FIRST, to the credit of the Principal and Interest Fund for the Bonds and any Parity Bonds 
in the manner set forth above under “Principal and Interest Fund;” 
SECOND, to the credit of any Reserve Fund established in connection with Parity Bonds; 
THIRD, to the credit of the Rebate Fund, the amounts described above under “Rebate 
Fund;” 
FOURTH, to the payment of any termination payment due and owing under any interest 
rate exchange agreement or swap entered into by the Enterprise; 
FIFTH, to the payment of the Debt Service Requirements of Subordinate Securities, 
including any reasonable reserves established therefor; and 
SIXTH, after the payments and accumulations set forth in FIRST through FIFTH above, 
any remaining Pledged Revenues may be used for any lawful purpose. 
Additional Covenants .  The City makes certain covenants relating to, but not limited to, the 
efficient operation and maintenance of the System, restrictions on the disposal of property comprising the 
System, keeping accounts and records relating to the funds referred to in the Bond Ordinance, and tax 10 
covenants, pursuant to the Bond Ordinance.  See APPENDIX A under the caption “SUMMARY OF 
ORDINANCE PROVISIONS—Covenants” for a description of such covenants. 
Ordinance Irrepealable.  The Bond Ordinance provides that after any of the Bonds are issued, such 
ordinance shall remain irrepealable, but amendable, until the Bonds and the interest accruing thereon shall 
have been fully paid, cancelled or discharged.

RISK FACTORS 
THE PURCHASE OF THE BONDS IS SUBJECT TO CERTAIN RISKS.  EACH PROSPECTIVE 
INVESTOR IN THE BONDS IS ENCOURAGED TO READ THIS OFFICIAL STATEMENT IN ITS 
ENTIRETY.  PARTICULAR ATTENTION SHOULD BE GIVEN TO THE FACTORS DESCRIBED 
BELOW WHICH, AMONG OTHERS, COULD AFFECT THE PAYMENT OF THE PRINCIPAL OF 
AND INTEREST ON THE BONDS, AND COULD ALSO AFFECT THE MARKET PRICE OF THE 
BONDS TO AN EXTENT THAT CANNOT BE DETERMINED. 
Limited Obligations 
The Bonds do not constitute a lien upon any real or personal property of the City.  Rather, the Bonds 
constitute an irrevocable pledge of a lien (but not necessarily an exclusive lien) upon the Pledged Revenues, 
which are at all times senior and superior to the lien thereon of the Subordinate Securities.  The payment of 
the principal of and interest on the Bonds is dependent on the City’s receipt of Income from the System.  
Owners may not look to any general or other revenues of the City, including without limitation the proceeds 
of ad valorem taxes, for the payment of the principal of and interest on the Bonds, and the Bonds do not 
constitute a general obligation of the City. 
Issuance of Parity Bonds  
The City has the right to issue additional bonds payable from the Pledged Revenues and secured 
by a lien on the Pledged Revenues on a parity with, but not superior to, the lien of the Bonds; however, 
specific conditions and requirements which are set forth in an additional bonds test in the Bond Ordinance 
must be met by the City prior to the issuance of such Parity Bonds.  For so long as the 2020 CWRPDA 
Loan remains outstanding, the City is also required to meet the coverage test provided by the 2020 Loan 
Agreement prior to issuing additional Parity Bonds.  See “THE BONDS—Security for the Bonds—
Additional Bonds” and APPENDIX A under the caption “SUMMARY OF ORDINANCE PROVISIONS—
Priorities; Liens; Issuance of Parity Bonds— Issuance of Parity Bonds .”  Debt service on the Bonds, the 
Outstanding Parity Obligations, and any future Parity Bonds will be payable from the Pledged Revenues 
on a pro-rata basis.  Accordingly, to the extent that future obligations are issued on a parity with the lien of 
the Bonds, the security for the Bonds may be diluted.  See “THE SYSTEM—System Capital Improvements 
and Long-Term Planning—Anticipated CIP Funding” for a discussion of currently anticipated future bond 
issuances.  The City currently anticipates (but cannot guarantee) that future Pledged Revenues will be 
sufficient to meet the issuance requirements for any future Parity Bonds.   
Enforceability of Owners’ Remedies Upon Default 
In the event of a default in the payment of principal of or interest on the Bonds, there is no provision 
for acceleration of maturity of the principal of the Bonds.  Consequently, remedies available to registered 
owners and Beneficial Owners of the Bonds may have to be enforced from year to year.  Moreover, there 
is no bond trustee or similar person or entity to monitor or enforce the provisions of the Bond Ordinance 
on behalf of the registered owners and Beneficial Owners of the Bonds, and therefore such registered 
owners and Beneficial Owners of the Bonds should be prepared to enforce such provisions themselves if 
the need to do so ever arises.  A description of what constitutes an Event of Default under the Bond 
Ordinance and the remedies therefor can be found in APPENDIX A under the captions “SUMMARY OF 
ORDINANCE PROVISIONS—Default Provisions and Remedies of Owners.” 
The remedies available to the owners of the Bonds upon a default are in many respects dependent 
upon judicial action, which is often subject to discretion and delay under existing constitutional law, 
statutory law and judicial decisions, including specifically the federal Bankruptcy Code.  The legal opinions 13 
to be delivered concurrently with delivery of the Bonds will be qualified as to enforceability of the various 
legal instruments by limitations imposed by bankruptcy, reorganization and insolvency or other similar 
laws affecting the rights of creditors generally, now or hereafter in effect; as to usual principles of equity 
which may limit the specific enforcement under State law of certain remedies; as to the exercise by the 
United States of America of the powers delegated to it by the federal Constitution; and as to the reasonable 
and necessary exercise, in certain exceptional situations, of the police power inherent in the sovereignty of 
the State and its governmental bodies in the interest of serving an important public purpose.   
Factors that May Cause Insufficiency of Expected Revenues 
The generation of Pledged Revenues is dependent upon several factors outside the City’s control, 
such as the economy, collection of fees, continued growth (or lack thereof) and changes in law.  It is 
impossible to predict whether current economic conditions will continue, better or worsen, or to predict 
how future conditions will affect the operation of the System or the Enterprise’s finances in general.  The 
following factors, among others, may impact the generation of Pledged Revenues in the future. 
General.  Payment of the Bonds is dependent upon the generation of sufficient Pledged Revenues 
of the System.  If the System becomes inoperable due to damage, destruction, environmental or 
governmental restriction or for any other reason, if the System should lack raw water or lack treatable water 
due to contamination, lack of adequate supply to serve existing customers due to drought or for any other 
reason, if the System has inadequate storage or transmission facilities, if the City is unable to increase rates 
and charges for any reason or if the Enterprise incurs unanticipated expenses or reduced revenues due to 
power rate increases or for any other reason, the City may be unable to generate adequate revenues from 
the System to pay debt service on the Bonds.  No assurance can be made that the generation of Pledged 
Revenues from the System will be sufficient to pay debt service on the Bonds, even in observation of the 
rate maintenance covenant provided in the Bond Ordinance.  See “THE BONDS—Security for the Bonds—
Rate Maintenance Covenant.” 
Environmental Considerations.   The amount of available water depends on many factors, 
including water quality, economics, delivery system restrictions, aquifer depletion, and maintenance of 
adequate carry-over storage for droughts.  As with all other water providers, the City’s water supply is 
subject to environmental conditions resulting from the semi-arid climate of the State, and the water supply 
is impacted by drought from time to time.  Drought conditions may have an impact on the System’s ground 
water supply, which could potentially cause a materially adverse impact on water sales.  Customer behavior 
may also impact water sales.  Regardless of current conditions, customers may continue to use less water 
each year in response to previous droughts, water restrictions, indoor conservation/efficiency and increased 
water rates.   
Fluctuations in Water Revenues.  Fluctuations in water revenues can be exacerbated by the fixed 
nature of the System’s costs as the fixed cost of providing water does not vary considerably when the 
amount of water delivered to customers varies.  The inconsistency between a fixed cost structure and a 
variable revenue structure causes annual budgeting and financial instability and uncertainty.  It is not 
possible to predict what impacts, if any, such inconsistency might have on future System revenues. 
Environmental Regulations 
The operation of the System, including the administration of its sources of supply, is subject to 
substantial environmental protection regulation under local, State and federal law.  Due to the ever-
changing environmental regulatory environment, there can be no assurance that new laws and regulations 
or new interpretations of existing laws and regulations will not frustrate or delay otherwise feasible projects 
or result in increased capital and operating expenses for the System.   14 
Future Changes in Laws 
In addition to the various federal, state and local laws that apply to the operation of, and liability 
relating to, the operation of water and sewer systems (see “—Environmental Regulations” above), various 
Colorado and federal laws and regulations also apply to the obligations created by the issuance of the Bonds.  
There can be no assurance that there will not be changes in interpretation of, or additions to, the applicable 
laws and regulations which would have a material adverse effect, directly or indirectly, on the operation of 
the System or the Enterprise, or on the general affairs of the City. 
THE SYSTEM 
The Enterprise 
The Enterprise was established in 1994 by City ordinance, as later amended in 2014.  The City 
Council serves as the governing body of the Enterprise, and the officers of the City Council and of the City 
serve as officers and the governing body of the Enterprise.  Powers of the Enterprise include, but are not 
limited to, the power to issue revenue bonds in the manner in which City revenue bonds may be issued 
without voter approval in advance and to pledge any revenues of the System to the payment of such revenue 
bonds.  The City is to continue to own the assets of the System, acquire and convey properties constituting 
part of the System, employ and discharge the officers, managers and employees of the System, keep books 
and records relating to the System, invest and manage funds, budget and appropriate revenues and 
expenditures relating to the System, fix, adjust and collect water and sewer rates, fees, tolls and charges and 
tap fees, and prescribe rules and regulations relating to the System.  See “APPENDIX D—THE CITY—
Constitutional Amendment Limiting Taxes and Spending” for a discussion of the TABOR amendment. 
The performance of the obligations of the Enterprise under the Bond Ordinance is not subject to 
the limitations of TABOR so long as the Enterprise continues to qualify as an “enterprise” under TABOR.  
According to Bond Counsel, if the System is disqualified as an enterprise under TABOR, (i) the City may 
continue to impose and increase fees, rates, and charges of the System without voter approval but subject, 
however, to the refund requirements of TABOR; (ii) all Pledged Revenues used to pay debt service on the 
Bonds will be excluded from the refund requirements of TABOR; and (iii) if the City is required to reduce 
spending in order to comply with its fiscal year spending limit under TABOR, the City will first be required 
to reduce spending for purposes for which it does not have an obligation under law or by contract prior to 
reducing spending required to comply with the other covenants contained in the Bond Ordinance. 
Generally 
The System’s long-term direction has been determined over the years by a focus on the key mission 
components of the System: water resources and treatment; sewer facilities and treatment; reclaimed water 
facilities and treatment; long-term planning for capital and operating needs; rate setting and financing 
methods; and overall financial management of the System.  The System serves approximately 33,634 water 
and 32,060 sewer customers (as of the end of 2023).  The City’s Public Works and Utilities Department 
staff administers the System and is currently staffed with approximately 211 full time equivalent employees 
per the adopted 2024 budget.   
System Management 
The management of the System is established by the Public Works and Utilities Department, with 
guidance from the City Manager’s Office and review and approval by the City Council.  As part of a 2023 
restructuring, management within the Public Works and Utilities Department was reorganized.  The most 
significant change involved moving the City Engineer and associated engineering staff in Community 15 
Development to the Public Works and Utilities Department.  The reimagined City Engineer role took 
ownership of all construction projects within the City, whether horizontal or vertical, regardless of function 
or funding source (i.e., water utility, sewer utility, stormwater utility, general facility, general transportation 
infrastructure, etc.).  
The engineering teams from Public Works and Utilities and Community Development were 
combined within one division in Public Works and Utilities with new opportunities to engage in a wider 
set of projects.  Additionally, capital projects staff from Facilities Management and pavement management 
staff from Streets Operations were moved under the new Assistant City Engineer-Civil/Citywide section 
within the new consolidated City Engineering Division in Public Works and Utilities.  The Water Resources 
and the Water Quality teams, as well as GIS and asset management from Community Development and 
Public Works and Utilities were moved under the City Engineering Division as well.  
The City Engineer, along with the Operations Manager and Utilities Manager, report to the Public 
Works and Utilities Director.  A brief description of their responsibilities and short biography are outlined 
below.  
Public Works and Utilities Director.  The Public Works and Utilities Director is responsible for 
organizing and directing the City of Westminster's Public Works and Utilities Department that consists of 
an administrative section and three divisions:  Operations; Utilities; and City Engineering.  Work involves 
improving quality of life in Westminster through sound management of water, sewer, reclaimed water, and 
storm utilities; streets and transportation networks; City facilities; and the City’s fleet.  The Public Works 
and Utilities Director will accomplish department directives through the direct and indirect supervision of 
all personnel assigned to the department.  
Paul Knippel joined the City of Westminster in November 2023 following a 14-year engagement 
as the Director of Public Works and Engineering for the City of Frisco, Texas.  Mr. Knippel has 35 years’ 
experience in design, construction, and operation of public infrastructure, including 24 years as a municipal 
public works administrator.  Mr. Knippel is a Registered Professional Engineer in the State of Texas and 
holds a Bachelor of Science in Civil Engineering from Texas A&M University and a Master of Public 
Administration from the University of Texas at Arlington. 
Public Works and Utilities Operations Manager .  The Public Works and Utilities Operations 
Manager is responsible for the overall management of the operation of Street Operations, Fleet, and 
Facilities.  The manager reports to the Director of Public Works and Utilities.  The City is currently in the 
process of hiring a new Public Works and Utilities Operations Manager.  
Utilities Manager .  The Utilities Manager is responsible for the overall management of the 
operation and maintenance of the City’s sewer collection and water distribution system, as well as the 
operation and maintenance of two water treatment facilities, a sewer treatment facility and a reclaimed 
water treatments facility.  Under the direction of the Public Works and Utilities Director, the Utilities 
Operation Manager is responsible for budget development and tracking, short and long-range capital 
improvement project planning and ensuring the City is compliant with local, state and federal water, sewer 
and reclaimed water treatment regulations.  Work also involves planning, controlling, supervising and 
evaluating the daily operations for the Division.  
Andrea Song has over 26 years of experience in the water industry in both engineering and 
operations.  This experience is a combined 15 years of public utility operations leadership, and 11 years 
consulting engineering experience.  Ms. Song is a nationally recognized leader in optimization practices for 
the water sector.  Hired in December 2021, she leads a staff of 110 water, sewer, and utility billing 
professionals in the Utilities Division of the Public Works and Utilities Department at the City.  Ms. Song 16 
holds a Bachelor of Science degree from Virginia Tech in Chemical Engineering and a Master of Science 
in Environmental Engineering from Johns Hopkins University.  Ms. Song is a registered professional 
engineer, Class A Certified Water Treatment Operator, and Class 1 Distribution Operator in the State of 
Colorado.  Ms. Song also is a Project Management Professional (PMP), and she is currently serving as 
Director of the American Water Works Association. 
City Engineer .  The City Engineer is responsible for supervising public works, utilities and 
citywide civil engineering and technical staff and activities and is responsible for long and short-term capital 
and infrastructure planning, location, design, and construction of the City’s water, sewer, reclaimed water, 
stormwater, transportation, and facility assets, including in some cases operation and maintenance of certain 
of these assets.  
The City Engineer supervises three workgroups: 
• Utilities Engineering, under the direction of an Assistant City Engineer, which includes 
utilities infrastructure engineering, water resources, and water quality labs; 
• Citywide Civil Engineering, under the direction of an Assistant City Engineer, which 
includes transportation engineering, street infrastructure teams, and facilities capital 
project management; and 
• GIS which is responsible for citywide database analyses, mapping services, and asset 
management teams. 
John Burke worked for a small consulting firm for 10 years prior to joining the City in 2004.  As a 
project manager, John has invested 20 years with the City completing over $150 million dollars of 
infrastructure projects for the City, including Westminster Station and Downtown Westminster.  With the 
reorganization of the Public Works and Utilities Department, John was hired as the new City Engineer in 
December of 2023 and leads a staff of 62 professionals responsible for delivering $400 million dollars of 
infrastructure projects in the next 3 years.  Mr. Burke holds a Bachelor of Science degree from Colorado 
State University and is a registered professional engineer in the State of Colorado. 
Water Facilities
Water Supply and Planning.  The City values a resilient, high-quality water supply, and plans and 
applies resources accordingly.  Planning for future water supply to meet anticipated demand is a continuous 
process that combines technical, scientific information and policy direction.
All of the City’s drinking water supply is conveyed through Standley Lake, and the majority of that 
water originates from the mountain snowpack in the Clear Creek watershed.  The City shares storage in 
Standley Lake with the cities of Thornton, Northglenn, and the Farmers Reservoir and Irrigation Company 
(“FRICO”).  The City owns water rights to use Standley Lake to store water from Clear Creek, Coal Creek, 
and the Fraser River.  The City also has secured water rights to exchange lower-quality water released from 
downstream reservoirs for additional high-quality water diverted to Standley Lake.  The City’s water rights 
portfolio alone is valued at over one billion dollars at today’s market rates.  
The City has taken a protective and proactive approach to ensuring an adequate water supply will 
be available to support the City into the future.  The results of this work comprise the City’s Water Supply 
Plan (“WSP”).  The WSP lays out a strategy to provide a dependable supply of high-quality water to the 
City, Federal Heights, and various unincorporated areas in Adams and Jefferson Counties at the planned 
build-out of the system.  City staff uses a sophisticated, proprietary computer model that incorporates build-17 
out water demand estimates based on the City’s most current Comprehensive Land Use Plan, data from 
complex court decrees, paleohydrology identified through tree ring records, and historic water call records 
among other items.  This data is evaluated through a probabilistic approach that is used to determine long-
term water supply and demand.  This model is also used to evaluate various climate change models to 
ensure the City is prepared for changes in water supply as a result of changes in temperature and 
precipitation.   
Through this updated probabilistic modeling process, it has been identified that the 1950s drought 
of record utilized by many Denver-area utilities is only an “average” worst drought, meaning that there is 
a significant likelihood of more serious droughts than what is seen in documented streamflow records over 
the last 80 years.  Fortunately, the City’s water supply system is planned to dependably meet the current 
and future needs of its customers during hydrologic conditions similar to those of Clear Creek’s paleo 
record, going back more than 450 years.  
The City continually coordinates updates to the WSP and the Comprehensive Land Use Plan to 
ensure long-range land use planning is appropriately supported by the WSP.  The City roughly estimates 
City build-out around the year 2040.  Continued water supply development is required to ensure support 
for development in currently undeveloped land, revitalization of existing developments, and for continued 
resiliency of the water supply system.  The City also closely evaluates water savings from anticipated 
passive indoor and outdoor conservation and actively evaluates other mechanisms to maximize available 
water supplies.   
The current WSP ensures adequate water to provide for growth through the year 2040 at the current 
rate of the City’s growth.  According to City officials, there is currently a sufficient water supply for existing 
needs, and there are clearly defined long-range master plans that identify how the City will ultimately meet 
future needs. 
Along with water supply system capital improvement projects, the City’s water supply is ensured 
by continuing diligence in protecting the City’s water rights in court.  The City holds senior water rights in 
Colorado’s Water Division 1, and City staff monitor water activity and proposed decrees to protect these 
rights.  The City will also continue to opportunistically file for junior rights and exchanges that increase the 
firm yield of the City’s water supply.  Water supply for the City’s post-buildout population will depend 
upon a number of variables including density, plumbing fixture and irrigation technology, and types of 
commercial and industrial use that are present at that time.  Because the City is a leader in the integration 
of water supply and land use planning, these variables are constantly monitored and adjustments to the 
WSP are made as needed. 
Through the implementation of the current WSP and managing future development with purposeful 
water allocation, the City will continue to be a well-planned, sustainable water utility sufficient to enhance 
the City and provide vital flows during severe droughts.  18

Raw Water Storage .  The City currently has three main storage vessels for raw water, namely 
Standley Lake, Jim Baker Reservoir, and the West Gravel Lakes.  Standley Lake stores all raw water used 
for treatment and distribution throughout the City.  Jim Baker Reservoir and the West Gravel Lakes are 
used to pay returns and operate water exchanges.  A future storage vessel for operating exchanges and 
paying returns is planned near the town of Wattenberg adjacent to the South Platte River.  The City owns 
approximately 22,500 acre-feet of storage in Standley Lake.  The rest of the 43,000 acre-foot lake is owned 
by the cities of Thornton and Northglenn, and FRICO, who, together with the City form the Standley Lake 
Operating Committee (“SLOC”).    
Water Quality.  Several measures are in place to protect Standley Lake from negative water quality 
impacts.  These include the Woman Creek Reservoir, which captures all runoff from the Rocky Flats 
National Wildlife Refuge (formerly the Rocky Flats Environmental Technology Site or “RFETS”) and 
prevents it from entering Standley Lake; the Kinnear Ditch Pipeline which passes Coal Creek water around 
the RFETS to Standley Lake; the Church Ditch Water Quality Project which captures stormwater runoff 
and diverts it around Standley Lake; and wetlands which treat overland flow before it enters Standley Lake.  19 
On top of these measures, recreation activities at Standley Lake are limited and regulated to protect the 
water quality.   
For many years the City has acted to protect the water quality in Clear Creek and Standley Lake.  
This has been accomplished through water quality rulemakings before the Water Quality Control 
Commission through actively engaging and collaborating with a variety of partners in the Clear Creek 
Basin.  The City operates a robust, real-time water quality monitoring program in Clear Creek and in 
Standley Lake which it operates with partner cities and organizations.  The City is leading a Source Water 
Protection Plan that evaluates salts in the watershed and is incorporating input and cooperation from other 
municipalities, counties, the State, and other organizations.  The City is also participating in a joint 
watershed protection planning effort to identify wildfire mitigation projects in the Clear Creek Basin which 
is of paramount importance to the City’s water supply. 
The City has a responsibility on behalf of the public to deliver safe and clean water from Colorado’s 
high mountains to each drinking water tap.  Recognizing this responsibility, the City has consistently 
worked to establish programs for the protection of the water quality in Standley Lake.   
The EPA is working to implement new regulations to establish nationwide drinking water quality 
standards.  On April 10, 2024, the EPA announced the finalized National Primary Drinking Water 
Regulation, establishing legally enforceable levels called Maximum Contaminant Levels (“MCLs”) for 
certain Per-and Polyfluoroalkyl Substances (“PFAS”) in drinking water.  The new rules prevent companies 
from starting or resuming the manufacture or processing of numerous PFAS without a complete EPA 
review and risk determination.  The EPA also finalized health-based, non-enforceable Maximum 
Contaminant Level Goals (“MCLGs”) for certain PFAS.  Under the new regulations, public water systems 
must monitor for certain PFAS and have three years (by 2027) to complete initial monitoring, with ongoing 
compliance monitoring required thereafter.  Water systems must also provide the public with information 
on the levels of PFAS in their drinking water beginning in 2027 and have until 2029 to implement solutions 
that reduce PFAS, if monitoring shows that drinking water levels exceed the MCLs.  The City analyzed its 
finished water quarterly in the first half of 2024 for 29 PFAS compounds, as regulatorily required, and no 
PFAS compounds were detected in the raw and treated samples.  With no detection of PFAS compounds, 
technology to treat PFAS is not currently needed and does not exist at the System.  Although the City does 
not currently have reason to suspect the occurrence of problematic levels of PFAS in its drinking water, the 
System will be subject to, and will need to comply with, the new regulations. 
Water Treatment Plants.  The City’s raw water supply is treated prior to distribution at one of the 
two existing treatment plants owned and operated by the City.  These two plants currently supply a 
combined treated water capacity of 59 million gallons per day (“MGD”).  In 2023, the maximum daily 
production was 30 MGD and the average daily flow was 15 MGD.  Upon completion of the Project, the 
new water treatment plant is expected to increase the System’s combined treated water capacity to 73.7 
MGD, with additional planned expansions of the newest plant continuing to increase capacity in the future.  
See “THE BONDS—Application of Bond Proceeds— The Project .”  The Northwest Water Treatment 
Facility, a membrane filtration plant, was constructed in 2002 and is a 15 MGD facility.  The Semper Water 
Treatment Plant, a dual media filtration plant, was constructed in 1970, with subsequent expansions 
bringing the plant’s total capacity to 44 MGD.  The three plants will serve the System until such time as 
the Semper Water Treatment Plant is decommissioned.  The decommissioning of the Semper Water 
Treatment Plant is not currently expected to happen for at least 20 years, until such time as the new water 
treatment plant is built out to full capacity. 
Treated Water Storage and Distribution Facilities .  Ten treated water storage facilities are 
operated by the City with a total capacity of 31.5 million gallons (“MG”).  The water system is divided into 
14 pressure zones, and water pressure is maintained by 10 pumping stations and by gravity flow.  The 20 
present service area is served by a transmission and distribution system consisting of approximately 544 
miles of water mains ranging in size from 1” to 60” in diameter.  Approximately 24% of the water 
distribution lines are constructed of cast/ductile iron, 24% is asbestos-cement (“A/C”) pipe, 42% is 
polyvinyl chloride (“PVC”) and 10% is considered “other.”  No significant problems with leakage have 
been detected; the System’s billed ratio is within the American Water Works Association norms.  The 
weighted age of the distribution pipe is approximately 27 years. 
Sanitation Facilities   
General.  The City’s sewer collection facilities capture sewer flows from the user and convey 
sewage to the treatment facilities.  Collection infrastructure is comprised of gravity sewer, force main sewer, 
seven lift stations, four sewer diversion stations, and three primary sewer siphons.  The City’s sewer 
collection system is segregated into two separate sewer sub-basins, Big Dry Creek basin and Little Dry 
Creek basin.  Sanitary flows from the Big Dry Creek sub-basin are collected and discharged to the City’s 
Big Dry Creek Sewer Treatment Facility, while sanitary flows from the Little Dry Creek sub-basin are 
collected and discharged through a metering station into the Metro Water Recovery transmission system 
that flows to Metro Water Recovery’s Central Sewer Treatment Plant.  Approximately 75% of the City’s 
sewer customers are served by the City’s sewer facilities and 25% of the City’s sewer customers receive 
sewer service from Metro Water Recovery under a contract with the City. 
Collection.  Sewer infrastructure is separated into two categories, sewer collection and sewer 
treatment.  The collection system includes gravity sewer piping, force main sewer piping, sewer lift stations 
and control structures, and sanitary sewer devices.  There are currently seven lift stations in operation within 
the City.  There are four concrete diversion structures that can be utilized to control flow to downstream 
pipe sections and three sewer siphons that convey sewer across Big Dry Creek at three different locations. 
The collection system consists of approximately 433 miles of sewer lines constructed of PVC pipe, 
vitrified clay pipe, concrete, ductile iron, cast iron, and high-density polyethylene pipe (“HDPE”).  The 
seven lift stations are needed to serve approximately 5% of the service area with collection of sewer in the 
remainder of the service area accomplished by gravity flow.  In 2013, the City started maintaining the sewer 
collection system in-house.  This maintenance program consists of jet cleaning and television inspection of 
1/3 of the collection system annually.  The City monitors four permitted industries to comply with federal 
pretreatment regulations.  Three of these industries discharge pretreated sewer to the City collection system 
and one is a zero-discharge industry.  No other accounts of the City are considered potential pretreatment 
customers.  The City maintains separate sewer collection and storm sewer systems.  
Treatment.  Big Dry Creek is a biological sewer treatment plant with biological nutrient removal 
for phosphorous and nitrogen and uses ultraviolet light for disinfection.  The plant has a design capacity for 
9.8 MGD.  In 2023, the maximum daily flow was 12.75 MG and the average daily flow was 6.45 MG.   
The sewer treatment plant serves customers located in the Big Dry Creek Basin portion of the City’s 
service area.  Sewer effluent is discharged into Big Dry Creek or into the Reclaimed Water System during 
the summer reuse period.  Because the sewer facilities discharge effluent into Big Dry Creek and the 
Reclaimed Water System, a discharge permit is required under provisions of the Federal Clean Water Act 
and the Colorado Water Quality Control Act.  This permit regulates the amount of pollutants in the effluent.  
The Enterprise’s current discharge permit from the State of Colorado’s Department of Public Health and 
Environment (“CDPHE”) went into effect on January 1st, 2020.  The City continues to remain in compliance 
with all material terms of the discharge permit, with three exceptions in 2023 for one ammonia exceedance, 
one total inorganic nitrogen exceedance (related to the construction of new process equipment), and one 
flow exceedance due to extreme rainfall.21 
Sewer treatment is provided to customers in the Little Dry Creek area of the City’s service area by 
Metro Water Recovery pursuant to a perpetual service contract executed January 1, 1964.  The City pays a 
flat rate, as determined by the contract, to Metro Water Recovery, and bills each customer individually 
according to the City’s current rate schedule.  Enterprise officials state that sufficient capacity is available 
in the existing interceptor lines and treatment plant to serve the development projected in the area served 
by Metro Water Recovery.  The dividing line for which the plant will provide the sewer service is 
approximately 92nd Avenue.  See “CITY MAP.”  The City anticipates growth in the area served by Metro 
Water Recovery in future years now that the Regional Transit District’s B Line has been completed.  This 
commuter rail line is located in the southern part of the City and will provide direct service from Denver’s 
Union Station to the City.  Tap fees for this area are collected by the Enterprise and the portion that is due 
to Metro Water Recovery is remitted to them. 
Reclaimed Water Treatment Facility and Distribution System .  In 2000, the City completed the 
construction of the Reclaimed Water Treatment Facility, located adjacent to the City’s sewer treatment 
facility.  This facility further filters and disinfects the treated sewer from the Big Dry Creek Sewer 
Treatment Facility before delivering it for use as a landscape irrigation source to golf courses, parks, right 
of ways, commercial properties, and residential common areas within the City.  The Reclaimed Water 
Distribution System consists of 26 miles of piping that serves approximately 137 customers.  Customers 
are charged 80% of the equivalent potable water rate, and fees for new reclaimed taps are similarly 20% 
less than the potable equivalent.  The Reclaimed Water Treatment Facility runs seasonally, mid-March 
through mid-November. 
The Reclaimed Water Treatment Facility was expanded in 2012 to increase the treatment capacity 
to 10 MGD.  On average, the system supplies 1,500 AF of water a year, approximately 5% of the City’s 
current water demand.  As a significant component of the City’s water supply, the reclaimed system helps 
fully utilize reusable water rights, demonstrating the City’s dedication to environmental and economic 
stewardship.  The reclaimed water program and its water quality comply with all the applicable standards 
established by the CDPHE.   
Biosolids Disposal Facility.   In 1996, the City acquired approximately 2,700 acres of farmland 
near Strasburg, Colorado that is utilized as the Natural Resource Farm for biosolids reuse.  The City 
purchased an additional 300 acres in 2001.  In 2019, the City purchased an additional approximately 1,200 
acres in Arapahoe County which is permitted for biosolids application.  This purchase further diversified 
City-owned lands available for biosolids application to provide more flexibility and reliability for the 
application of biosolids for beneficial use.  These facilities operate in conjunction with the Big Dry Creek 
Facility.  Biosolids generated from the sewer process are collected and transported to the site for soil 
conditioning.  The City utilizes three tractor trucks, three trailers, a front-end loader, a John Deere tractor, 
and a “slinger” trailer to apply biosolids.  
System Capital Improvements and Long-Term Planning   
Long-Term Capital Planning.  A critical element of the City’s Public Works and Utilities 
Department is to plan, prioritize, and implement Capital Improvement Projects (“CIPs”) to responsibly 
engineer the City’s $4 billion utility assets for a strong economy and vibrant community health.  Included 
in this scope is proactively repairing or replacing existing infrastructure to protect public health and safety 
now and in the future, maintaining responsible and defensible budgets, and supporting a financially 
sustainable enterprise.  To meet these goals, the City implements an extensive long-term planning process 
and maintains an asset data base to assess risk and criticality to prioritize CIPs.  Long term planning for the 
assets is linked to planning for water and sewer rates and tap fees which are set to equitably recover System 
costs, fund capital projects and fund the System’s reserve accounts per City policy. 22 
Utility CIPs must be anticipated, planned, and budgeted over scheduled periods of time that are 
realistic and sustainable relative to tap fee sales, water and sewer rate revenue generation, and other revenue 
sources.  For the past fourteen years, staff has conducted long-term planning to accomplish this goal.  A 
large database of system assets is used to identify a comprehensive repair and replacement program required 
to maintain current levels of water and sewer service to City customers.  Information is also compiled for 
improvements that anticipate City needs for future development, redevelopment, and regulatory-driven 
improvements to protect public health and safety now and in the future.  Overall, this information represents 
the foundation for long-term capital planning of recommended highest priority projects.  CIPs identified as 
part of this effort are then prioritized using a systematic approach based on the International Infrastructure 
Maintenance Manual.  The approach incorporates system and asset replacement costs, industry-standard 
useful life, risk and anticipated criticality of the asset, the City’s comprehensive operations and maintenance 
programs, and other critical information.  By pursuing a proactive program to assess the needs and priorities 
of the utility system, and focusing funding on the highest priorities, the cost of operating and maintaining 
the utility system can be minimized by avoiding costly system failures, emergency response, and 
interruption of service.    
The City’s infrastructure database information is updated on 2-year intervals with the latest update 
completed in 2024.  This information sets forth water, sewer and reuse system valuations to assist the City 
in the analysis and evaluation of current user charges, fees, rates and related financial purposes.  The City 
maintains a financial model for financial planning purposes.  This model has assisted staff with planning 
for both short- and long-term needs, including the System’s operating requirements, cash flows, 
infrastructure needs (the repair, replacement, improvement or expansion of the System’s infrastructure) and 
the System’s rate stabilization and capital project reserves.  The use of this model also assists staff with 
financing projections, rate setting and the establishment of tap fees. 
Reserve Policies.  In 2006, City Council adopted reserve policies as a means to ensure long-term 
financial sustainability of the System.  The implementation of these policies has led to a defined and 
adequately funded Rate Stabilization Reserve and Capital Project Reserve as described below:   
Rate Stabilization Reserve:  This fund is used to mitigate revenue shortfalls due to weather related 
issues such as extreme rain during a season, drought conditions, or other variables that might affect water 
or sewer sales.  The target balance is calculated as 25% of water rate revenues and 10% of sewer revenues 
(based on budgeted annual revenues).  During years of better-than-average water sales, revenue surpluses 
are deposited into this account.  Once the reserve balance reaches 140% of the targeted amount, funds are 
allocated to the Capital Project Reserve.  If the reserve balance dips below 70% of the targeted amount, rate 
increases would be needed to provide sufficient revenues to reach the minimum target balance.  The Rate 
Stabilization Reserve was $15,355,401 as of December 31, 2023. 
Capital Project Reserve:  This fund was created to fund authorized capital improvement projects 
as part of the regular budget process, and may be used to fund emergency repairs to utility facilities as 
authorized by City Council.  The minimum balance is $3.0 million for the Water fund and $2.0 million for 
the Sewer Fund.  The maximum balance is calculated as 40% of the five-year adopted annual Capital 
Improvement Program (“CIP”) expenditures.  If the Capital Project Reserve balance falls below the 
minimum, rate increases and/or reducing the CIP shall be required.  If the balance exceeds the maximum, 
funding of the Capital Project Reserve will be reduced until the Capital Project Reserve falls below the 
maximum, which may include rate reductions.  The Capital Project Reserve was $76,746,616 as of 
December 31, 2023. 
Rates and Tap Fee Policy.  The City’s policy is to review and revise water and sewer rates and tap 
fees to ensure that costs are recovered equitably.  Rates are reviewed and recommendations for annual 
increases are made to City Council on an annual basis.  It is considered to be a best practice in the utilities 23 
industry to review, and when necessary, rebalance the rate and fees charged to ensure they are fair and 
equitable.   
In April 2017, City Council authorized a contract with Raftelis Financial Consultants (“RFC”) to 
perform a Cost of Service study to thoroughly evaluate aspects of the Utility including the existing water 
and sewer rates and fees, water use by customer type, and the value of the water and sewer utilities.  The 
City is planning to do a similar Cost of Service study in 2025.  
City Council adopts the rate increases during the annual budget cycle in the summer and fall with 
the following net revenue increases currently expected.  Upon adoption by City Council, rates take effect 
on January 1st of each year.  
2025 2026 
Net Water Revenue Increase 4.5% 4.5%
Net Sewer Revenue Increase 6.0 6.0
Rates are established to meet short-term cash flow needs for debt service, operations and 
maintenance, as well as for compliance with regulatory requirements.  They are also established to meet 
long-term requirements for infrastructure repair and replacement, debt service, and the funding of reserves 
per City policy.  
The City’s policy regarding tap fees has been to recover growth related capital costs for water 
resources and infrastructure costs.  Per City Code, tap fees automatically increase with the Engineering 
News Record inflationary index for construction costs; this policy has been in place since 2019.  
Periodically the utility conducts a thorough cost study to determine the most accurate water resources and 
infrastructure cost components and adjusts the tap fee accordingly.  This was also addressed in the 2017 
Cost of Service study and tap fees were subsequently adjusted as well.  See “—System Financial 
Information” below, for information regarding current rates and tap fees. 
Capital Improvements.  Repair and replacement projects are necessary for maintaining the integrity 
of the System’s infrastructure.  Improvement and expansion projects are also needed to address growth as 
the City moves toward build-out.  To address these needs, a five-year CIP is developed in conjunction with 
the annual budget process and updated every year to ensure that the appropriate projects are undertaken.  
Master Plans have been compiled for all aspects of the System to ensure that the infrastructure is routinely 
maintained in excellent condition.  These plans are regularly updated to assist with prioritizing 
infrastructure repairs, replacements and improvements.   
Anticipated CIP Funding . The Enterprise currently plans to pay for the System capital 
improvements identified in the CIP through a combination of Bond proceeds, legally available City funds, 
and the proceeds of future bond issuances.  The additional bond issuances are currently anticipated to occur 
in 2026, in 2027, and in 2029, subject to the then-current needs of the System and the satisfaction of the 
applicable additional bonds tests.  See “THE BONDS—Security for the Bonds—Additional Bonds.” 24

2025-2029 Capital Improvements Plan .  The Enterprise anticipates financing other capital 
improvements over the next five years with a combination of cash and debt financing as part of the annual 
budget preparation.  Historically, it has been a policy of management to use tap fees as the primary source 
of capital improvement funding.  This policy has allowed the Enterprise to expand and improve the System 
mostly on a pay-as-you-go basis rather than using additional bonded debt issues.  In the future, as the City 
approaches build-out (roughly anticipated to be around 2040), tap fee revenue will decline as development 
in the City slows.  Staff anticipates that at that time, rate revenues will be the primary funding source for

The City classifies its water and sewer customers as residential, commercial/industrial and

Water and Sewer Rate Schedule .  All water, sewer and reclaimed water customers connected to 
the Utility pay rates.  Except for fire hydrant use for municipal purposes and water used by the water 
department, all water delivered from the City’s water distribution system is metered, including water used 
by the City or departments thereof.  Sewer rates are based upon the quantity and quality of sewage collected.  
The minimum monthly rate for residential, including multiple unit dwellings, and public users is based on 
a 2,000 gallon/month minimum.  This is considered to be the “readiness to serve” charge, and ensures that 
each customer pays an appropriate amount to keep the sewer system ready to provide service at all times.  26 
Staff considers this to be similar to the fixed monthly water fee.  If the average monthly water consumption 
per user billed during the months of January through March is more than 2,000 gallons, then the monthly 
sewer use is multiplied by the per 1,000 gallon rate.  Reclaimed water is used solely for irrigation purposes, 
and the rate is set at 80% of potable water rates to provide an incentive for customers to connect to the 
City’s reclaimed system.  The average annual utility bill for the City in 2023 was $966. 
A new occupant of a residential unit is charged an initial sewer charge of $32.24 until water usage 
has been established.  Residential users who appeal the initial sewer charge rate may have the rate adjusted 
to the actual usage for the first four months of occupancy. 
The minimum monthly sewer charge for commercial users may be appealed to the Utility Billing 
Division for user charges resulting from the average monthly water billed during the period of January 
through March and may be adjusted if the water billed during the months of July through September 30 is 
less than the water billed during the months of January through March.  Commercial users may install a 
separate meter to measure outside water use for which no sewer charge will be assessed.  The City Council 
may adjust the period of time to be used to calculate the user charges when in its opinion climate conditions 
and water consumption patterns warrant such an adjustment.   
Water and Sewer Tap Fees.  Tap fees are one-time fees that developers are charged to connect to 
the City’s water and sewer systems.  They are based on the current value and size of the utility system and 
the value of the City’s water rights portfolio.  The structure of each tap fee is composed of components that 
together reflect the equitable portion of the utility system impacted by new customers.  As new customers 
connect to the system, they pay for the portion of the developed system they will use.  In this way, current 
customers benefit from lower water and sewer rates and increased reliability, while new customers pay their 
equitable share.  Current customers do not pay tap fees.  
Utility staff members calculate all non-single family residential tap fees individually to ensure that 
the City not only recovers the costs of providing access to the water and sewer systems, but to also ensure 
that customers are paying the appropriate tap fee for their building and its associated uses.  
Tap fee payments are required at the time that the building permit is issued.  The obligation to pay 
the fees for both water and sewer connections, until collected, constitutes a lien against the property.  Tap 
fees represent the customer’s investment in the existing capacity of the System and expansion.  The 
following table sets forth the tap fees collected by the City since 2019. 27

RFC performed a review of the infrastructure components of the City’s water and sewer tap fees 
as a part of their 2017 work for the City, provided recommendations, and City Council adopted these 
recommendations in 2017.  
Water Tap Fees.  A water tap fee consists of the following individual fees: a water resources fee, 
being the share of the cost to provide adequate raw water supply to be utilized by the tap; a treated water 
infrastructure fee, being the share of the utility system related to treating and distributing water to be utilized 
by the tap; the meter connection fee; and, when applicable, a fire connection fee.  To keep tap fees current 
in years when detailed reviews are not performed, the water tap fee and its individual components as 
described in the City Code are automatically increased in accordance with the annual construction cost 
index for construction costs as of December of each year, as reported in the construction economics section 
of the Engineering News-Record as published weekly by BNP Media.  Staff determined that this index is a 
more accurate representation of an inflator for construction-related work and materials. 
The water resources component is calculated in terms of Service Commitments (“SC”).  One SC is 
equal to 140,000 gallons of annual water use.  The infrastructure component is calculated based on a count 
of water fixtures in the building and the resulting meter size, which is the best determination of projected 
peak demand on the infrastructure system.   
The base water tap fee for each component is set forth in the following table.

Sewer Tap Fees.  The City’s sewer is treated by either the City-owned and operated Big Dry Creek 
Sewer Treatment Facility or Metro Water Recovery, depending on location.  Metro Water Recovery treats 
sewer generated by customers south of West 92nd Avenue.  The Big Dry Creek Sewer Treatment Facility 
treats sewer generated by customers north of 92nd Avenue.  All new customers must pay a tap fee to connect 
to one of these two facilities.  The City currently applies the Metro Water Recovery sewer treatment fee to 
the City’s overall sewer tap fee to keep development costs consistent.  
The sewer tap fee is based on two components:  the sewer transport fee; and sewer treatment fee.  
Sewer tap fees for both residential and non-residential new customers are based on a single-family 
residential equivalent (“SFRE”) methodology.  A 5/8” water tap represents one SFRE and larger tap sizes 
are scaled up in pricing depending on their proportional impact on the infrastructure system.  The base 
sewer tap fee for each SFRE is set forth in the following table.
